=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
If you are expecting a light, gimmicky repackaging of the parent game (as some such reincarnations are), you will be severely disappointed. 7W Duel stands on its own extremely well. The face up/face down/reveal-as-you-go mechanic is an innovative approach, enhanced by the ability to take a consecutive turn contained on many of the Wonders. The alternate victory conditions mean that instead of just trying to accumulate more points than your opponent, you must remain alert to the possibility that your hard-earned stack of civil buildings could be undone by a few science or military cards. While gameplay can feel a bit repetitive and the placement of cards can limit the decision space (many times the best move is obvious, giving the game a scripted feel), this is the finest of the 2p reimaginings of larger games that I have played.

Most of the scoring mechanics are similar to its parent game, but military uses a push-and-pull "Duel" type mechanic. I have to say, I usually hate that absolute form of shifting valuations in duel games, but here for some reason it works. I suspect this is because it is one aspect of the game, but not the most important. The fiance enjoyed this one as well, which means it will probably materialize in the house soon...

My fear with this one is replayability. I could see it dropping a point depending on how it holds up. The good news is, I'll probably play it enough to find out.

RATING: 9
Great two-player game. I think I prefer the open drafting used in Duel versus its big brother, 7 Wonders, as long-term planning is possible and necessary and decisions become more important (i.e. should I reveal something new, discard what he wants, or take what helps me the most?). This interactivity exists in the original but is at the forefront here. The possibility of an instant victory also looms large.

RATING: 9
Base game alone is an 8; bumps up to a 9 when played with the Pantheon expansion. 

I was a fan of the original 7 Wonders, but was dismayed that you had little to no effect on anyone not seated directly next to you. Which also meant a fair amount of down time at the larger play counts. In 7W:Duel however, [i]every[/i] action you take has an affect on your opponent. You also can't just ignore the military, as it's no longer just a way for your neighbour to score points, but one of two special victory conditions - both of which make for some surprising and exciting conclusions. You are forced to constantly adjust and monitory what your opponent is doing - which makes 7W:Duel so much more engaging on every level than it's predecessor. It's a solid, beautiful game that - despite its limited number of cards - always [i]feels[/i] like it plays out differently depending on the available cards, something that again can't be said of the original which starts to feel samey after a number of plays.

The Pantheon expansion further evolves the system by adding an additional option on your turns (from the 2nd era onwards), where careful planning of the card draft can be interrupted by purchasing a god instead. This minor fold throws a wrench into some of the more calculating aspects of the card draft, not to mention the advantages these gods provide. It extends playtime [i]slightly[/i] which may or may not be an issue, depending on your tastes. I would always prefer to play with Pantheon, but when introducing the game to new players I will avoid it as it's an expansion that's better enjoyed with respect to how it improves on the original system.

RATING: 8
The two-player rules of 7 Wonders were a bit of a strain. They obviously were an afterthought to increase the marketing appeal. After one session with them I never tried them again.

7 Wonders: Duel manages the feat to improve an already excellent base game into an outstanding two-player version that still feels like its parent but plays quicker and feels leaner while reducing the luck factor. My only issue with it is that it has to compete in a much tougher field of competitors than 7 Wonders that was one of the few games feasible for a large game group of 6+ players while not sidestepping to party games.

RATING: 8
Easier to set up for two than base 7 Wonders, card drafting changed, however the game still has similar feeling. I enjoyed the cards drafting from the pyramid. You can choose the card, which is better for you or deny one, which is good for your opponent as in 7 Wonders. But you can also choose a card which doesn't increase choice to your opponent, or which doesn't reveal a card, which is good for your opponent. You have to constantly think about it. It is definitely a better game for two.

Lengviau pasiruošti žaisti, negu originalų 7 Woders, kortelių pasirinkimas pakeistas, bet žaidimas vis dar yra pakankamai panašus. Man patinka kortų rinkimasis iš piramidės. RATING: 8.5
Excellent two-player game!  Very happy with this one, and I actually prefer this to the original 7 Wonders game, so far.  Nice and fast, with a clever "drafting" method with some exposed information, and some hidden.  There are three different win conditions, which keep players on their toes, since you can't ignore when your opponent is starting to exploit a strategy that may give them a sudden win.  Fast to play (30 minutes), which is also nice to have for those situations that call for it, since a lot of my two-player games are much longer.

I also got the metal military token, which was originally an Essen 2015 exclusive.  Very nice little addition to the game.  :-)

RATING: 8

While this would not be the case for everyone else, for me it's only downside is the limit of two players. I so wish there was a way of having this cater for more players right out of the box.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a good game, but it does have some annoying flaws:

-The quality of the age cards is quite poor. I don't mind them being small like some people do, but they're paper thin and could easily tear or get creases in them. They remind of the terrible Takenoko cards.

-I like my games' mechanics to really tie into their theme and I don't feel that 7WD's really do. For example, when in history has a civilization ever ruled over another due to science? Probably never. A commerce victory would have made more sense (for example, you win if you say have 30 coin more than your opponent). And it bugs me that you can't stockpile your resources like you can in other games or use your money to buy military. It's a good game, but you can't allow yourself to get too tied to the theme. If you're like me, try to think of it as an abstract game with a pasted on theme.

-I'm not much of a fan of asymmetrical games and it's because no matter how much a designer might say it's been balanced, I just can't believe them. With 7WD that's not a problem except for the wonders and even that's not too much of a problem because you're drafting them, but I don't feel like they're that balanced. For example, most of the wonders that allow you to play again right away are probably better than most of the ones that don't allow you to. And if you don't know the game well, you can easily draft them poorly and since success in the game is largely tied to your successful building of your wonders I think it's crucial that you draft them well. I would have given them all that power and, if necessary, made their other benefits weaker.

-I understand the expansion solves this problem, but with the base game I feel it's too easy to get put into a position, especially in the third age, where drawing anything is either a bad move or loses you the game. One could say, "you put yourself in that position", which might be true, but it isn't necessarily. While I'm sure many people like this aspect of the game, I think the base game would be better if you were allowed to pass, but not twice in a row. So if your opponent also passes you'd still have to make that bad move, but at least there'd be the chance that your opponent might decide it was in their best interests not to also pass. From the reviews of the expansion I've watched and read, the expansion might be worth it for the simple fact that it allows you to not take a card.

-The insert is garbage. Even with nothing else in the box, but the insert, the lid still won't fit over the box! Yeah, you could easily just throw it out, but I think this and the quality of the age cards just shows how disinterested Repos is in quality. I would think twice about getting another Repos game.

Overall, there are some good to great things about this game (the temples of the cards with some known and some unknown is absolutely brilliant), but I just think that if they had gone a little farther that the game would be more deserving of all the high praise it gets.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6.5
Wow. was I unimpressed by this one. I will play it a couple of more times but in my collection there are at least 50 two player games that I like more.

Reading the reviews and comments I believe there is more to it so it gets more chances. but in my two first games the game practically played itself. And errors were punished . 

Perhaps it has to do with my character. As an Intp I have to keep my options open and because it is a simple game these choices were to obvious.

The two instant winning conditions. Well if someone is strong on military chances are the other one is making it up in science. This sort of balance allthough it is adding tension really doesn't do it for me. The advantage given to the weaker military is a must because if it was different it could be that military ended the game at a start of an era. Science maybe I haven't had a look at the card distribution but it would be definately harder to pull off.
So the advantage of picking the start player by the weaker military is sort of prevent the game from ending catch up mechanism.

edit:
third play started throwing away military for money. Opponent did the same. This made it easier to build the wonders.  Gonna try something different next time.
Rating might go up.

Edit fourth play :
I don't play a lot of games more than 5 times and I am getting there quickly .
This time my opponent who started , did things to cripple me and played subpar for her own score.  She did score a lot of blue buildings and was omnipresent at the markets. I tried science and came one short , because  I couldn't manipulate the turn order because she crippled me in two resources. 
I start to wonder, no pun intended, if the amount of luck is not too high in this one. 
The buildings that she revealed were pretty much worthless to me. Okay at best.
While she got some really nice markets that I needed with next to it a building that required the esources from the market.
I had plenty of wood and stone but apparently these are not needed as much as I thought to build buildings.

Anyway I have seen the science route fail all four times now and the military is a much bigger threat.  
I am just going to read some comments. Play again military and see what happens.

I am not convinced that the best player wins . This has to do with the cards being revealed can be useless or very helpfull.

RATING: 8.4
7 Wonders, two player mode is a great two player game. It get tense when you want a card for yourself and ultimately exposing something for the other player. Before it's even shown, you just pray that nothing good is going to appear, but that's unlikely. Also if you do see what could be open, you say to yourself, and mabe out loud that wherever you do it's gonna hurt. There is a great back and forth, like a tug of war trying to out wit the other, to build your wonder, to get the matching symbols, to get the sciences, and to get the military. The only thing have to say is, maybe the regular cards should be bigger, other than that it's a great, somewhat fast, two player game.

RATING: 7.5
In many ways 7 Wonders Duel resembles its parent game 7 Wonders as over three ages players acquire cards that provide resources or advance their military or scientific development in order to develop a civilization and complete wonders.

What's different about 7 Wonders Duel is that, as the title suggests, the game is solely for two players, with the players not drafting cards simultaneously from hands of cards, but from a display of face-down and face-up cards arranged at the start of a round. A player can take a card only if it's not covered by any others, so timing comes into play as well as bonus moves that allow you to take a second card immediately. As in the original game, each card that you acquire can be built, discarded for coins, or used to construct a wonder.

RATING: 8
This is 7 wonders from the beginning to the end, but still lots of good additions and adjustments to make it a valid 2-player game. Bruno Cathala's touches are recognizable like the order you choose your wonders (see Mr. Jack player order) or taking clues from solitaire card games for limiting the available cards (see e.g. S.O.S. Titanic!). Moving the battle marker up and down is fine, the way guilds may benefit the other player is fine, the new - simpler but not less interesting - way for scoring green cards is also fine. Also the game is easier to teach and start playing than 7 Wonders was, as there are no hidden cards in hand and rules are also easier to understand for beginners.
I think it's a very well-designed one and I can just hope some expansions might be in stock to spice up things later...

RATING: 6
7 Wonders Duel takes place in three eras. In every era you can do scientific developments, strengthen your army and win coins to create a civilization and work wonders. There are three ways to win the game: military domination, scientific domination and civil victory. Military and scientific victories can take place throughout the game. The game will then also end immediately. If at the end of the third era no-one has won the game, the player with the most victory points wins a game in between, if you want to play something that does not take too long.

RATING: 7
The candle that burns the brightest burns the shortest, and 7 Wonders' candle burned oh so very bright. It was huge when it came out, and I named it my game of the year, and a couple months later it had vanished. It seemed everyone in my group got tired of it at once and no one seemed to feel the expansions added much except extra faff that gave the game a little more width, but little depth. My copy is long gone, and I'm not sure who else I know still has it.

Nevertheless, I was interested in this, but not enough to drop the cash on it outright. I have a person I regularly play 2p games with, but honestly we're spoiled for choice at this point and have way too many that don't get the the table often enough. I was glad to be able to try out someone else's copy.

I liked this. It distills the essence of the game, translates the drafting in an interesting way, and offers some neat choices. It's certainly better than the 2p variant of 7 Wonders, and moves at a nice pace. Glad I got to try it out.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.8
I can see why this one is a big hit.  It’s a basic set collection card game, but with enough variables and possibilities to give it a very strong strategic depth.  A Catan-like building mechanic combines with a solid card-drafting system and an interesting layout that lets you play defensively as well as offensively.  It’s also an excellent game for those of us who play a lot of two-player games but are looking for something a little more complex than most card games; it’s got Euro-style strategy in a small, compact, portable form.

It’s very much on point thematically, with beautiful art and a great design, and it’s surprising how much component-wise they manage to get into the small package.  There’s a bit of a learning curve to the rules, but it’s not incomprehensible.  Overall, just an excellent 2-player strategy game!

RATING: 4
This one really disappointed me.  I bought it solely based on the high recommendations of others.  Unfortunately this game has a serious flaw.  As you must draw cards that contain resource-producing  abilities, you can paint yourself into a corner (not by choice), if you don't come across the cards you need.  The game is played over the course of three rounds or 'ages'.  The first round seemed to be the best opportunity to get all the resources (clay, stone, wood, glass and papyrus).  I had everything but papyrus.  

That completely screwed me over.  By the end of the first age, I was playing from behind with no hope of recovering.  I do not like games where this is possible.  It would be like your high school soccer team playing Manchester United (but without the bragging rights of saying you played against Manchester United).  Why take a chance on playing a game like this if you can see your chances slipping away?  Once they're gone, this game just becomes a game of motions that you could inevitably lose.    I would consider giving it another chance, but this one just isn't in my wheelhouse.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Initial impressions: I'm afraid of replayability of this game since you only remove a few cards from each age. Overall I like it more than regular 7 Wonders. The combination of the game now being zero sum in terms of drafting and having to watch out for science and military victories makes the game seem to have a lot more interaction. I also like how there appear to be more things (at least compared to base game 7 Wonders, haven't played expansions) that have powers that feel less bland than say X gold or some scoring system: for example the science powers, the ability to mess with turn order with the extra turn with your wonders, etc. The spatial placement of the cards also adds another layer of should I take this but reveal these options for my opponent, and where each card appears affecting your tableau might provide the desired replayability. Riding a lot on the coattails on the 7 Wonders brand, but still a very solid 2 player game and if I do end up playing it more and it feels replayable I will bump this up to a 7.

RATING: 8
This is much more enjoyable for me than the original 7 Wonders.  
-There are more paths to victory.  It's extra exciting when you think you can pull off an instant win with military or science. 
-Includes both open and hidden information that influences the decisions you make.
-You have to balance what you take based on what may then become available for your opponent.  Makes for much tougher decisions.
-The wonders make Duel fill a bit more combo-y, especially with the ones that grant a 2nd turn.  Maybe I was never very good at building wonders in the original game?

Initial impressions are good and I see it having quite a bit of staying power.  Plays quickly, which is always a plus.  Don't see it taking over Battle Line as my favorite 2 player only game.  Not quite the same amount of tension is generated in Duel compared to BL.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I tried the original 7 Wonders once. It wasn't explained to me very well. The person ended up taking nearly every go for me as they probably felt it was quicker to do this than actually explain to me what I was meant to be doing so I could play the game. I was there so they could play. It was a bad experience and coloured my opinion of the game. I wasn't that interested in the game before this and I was even less interested afterwards. But I play a lot of 2 player games (Pagoda, Musée, Patchwork, Jambo, Jaipur, Lanterns: The Harvest Festival, Archaeology: The Card Game, Targi, Arboretum, Parade, San Juan, Morels, Biblios, Splendor, Isle of Skye: From Chieftain to King, Wir sind das Volk! I'm looking at you) so when this came along I wanted to try it.

It is good. After the first play of it I thought it would always play out in a similar manner. But as I have played it more I can see some further depth to it. It is an interesting tug-of-war game in which you need to dabble in a little bit of almost everything to firstly stop your opponent from winning, and then, have hopefully done enough to win yourself. 

It works on a very simple rules set. Pick a card from a pre-set card lay out (some are face down, some face up and if a card has no other cards overlapping it turns face up if not already). Similar to the base game there are 3 ages and 3 geometric lay outs for the cards. You then do one of three things with the card you picked. 1/ discard it for money. 2/ Pay/illustrate you have the cost in resources on the card and build it. 3/ Discard it under a wonder to build that.

There is much more than this to it in the free upgrades and special abilities of the cards. But it all works very well and is quick and easy to explain.

I guess it recreates the feelings of 7 Wonders for 2. I'm not the person to ask this, but I can tell you it is a good game and an enjoyable way to spend 30 minutes. All in a nice small box which is always handy! not the best two player game I have by far but it is good.

UPDATE

Still like this game a lot. But I still need to play it a few more times before I can form a final opinion on it. I do worry that possibly familiarity with the cards could dull the gaming experience, but I am no way near that point. Great two player game at the moment and one I would turn to before Jaipur, Jambo, Pagoda and Targi, but that is at the moment. With time we shall see! I have only played 7 Wonders once and didn't have a great experience so if you are holding back because you haven't tried the base game, don't!

Rating based on 2 players.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A great re-implementation of 7 Wonders for 2 players. One thing that always irked me about 7 wonders is that the resource management always felt so tight. You could never do everything you wanted. Duel feels a lot more open. You can do most of what you want to accomplish. Admittedly, this is more of a subjective feeling. A lot of players like the feeling of scarcity, and I normally don't mind it. But I much more prefer being able to accomplish most of your goals, without barely scraping by, and Duel give you that feeling. Aside from that, the rest of the game is great. There is a lot of "in your face" (obviously, given the title), but it's not overwhelming to me. Most of the things you do against your opponent are more in a roundabout way, instead of "give me that, it's mine now". I'd highly recommend this game to gamer buddies who enjoy 7 Wonders.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Positives: I like the addition of instant-win conditions (military and science), and the card stack revelation is a neat idea: more planning and less chance-based. Trading with bank is also good (though pricing is bizarre thematically-speaking). I also like how free builds are now indicated by icon instead of by text - much better interface!

Negatives: I still find the game more tactical than strategic (often making 2-way comparison choices, and just computing, which means much of the gameplay is simple computation), though the card revelation does mitigate this by adding a nice planning-ahead component which the original lacked. Still a few too many icons for my taste, but better than the original in this regard too.

Conclusion: If you like the original, you'll probably like this one and vice-versa. This one's a bit better if you want more strategy/planning, but in practice it will be decided by player count.

RATING: 6
Good:
The game has unique drafting mechanics that works really well and I hope to see the mechanic used in other titles.

Similar to 7 Wonders, the art and component quality are top notch. 

This nails 7 Wonder gameplay in a 2 player format. 

Bad:
A little too "thinky" for me. There is a lot of things to keep track of because a single overlooked/missed card or bit of information can lead to an unavoidable loss. As a result the turns tend to be drawn out while players check their setup, their opponents setup, currently available cards, and do a mental check of the cards that will be uncovered for your opponent for each drafted card.

RATING: 3
Unlike, it seems, the rest of the world, 7 Wonders hasn't been a huge part of my life. But we're always looking for a good 2p game.
The good: It's quick, lots of interesting decisions, clever setup.
The not sure: This has more confrontation than I really enjoy.
The bad: Am I the only person who struggles with the rules layout? I think I've worked out how to play, but it was definitely despite the very opaque rules booklet. And we struggled with the score pad. What is meant to go in each row? Again, we think we have worked it out. On the up-side, it is nice to see a space for the date.

After 7 plays, I'm starting to think I'm done with this. We find that the first player has a higher chance of winning, and it seems clear after the first few rounds who is likely to win. There are a number of destructive cards, which I dislike intensely - just as you get your engine rolling, things happen & you have to discard the cards or money you've been saving for that critical next card. I hope I know how to behave at the table, but the game leaves me feeling pouty and petulant. Even when I win! I think this is going to the trade table.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
[b]After 21 plays[/b] 

Still room for my rating to rise or fall depending on developing understanding. At the moment, the outcome seems too often decided by lucky draws in phase I, mostly resource cards. 4 of our last 5 games were end-to-end blowouts (which we split 2-2). We've analyzed these blowouts and as far as we can tell, the outcomes were down to lucky phase I draws.

There's still a chance our understanding is lacking in some way, and if we discover that to be so, my rating will probably rise.

A related gripe is military seems too weak. If you don't succeed at a military insta-win (which is hard and getting harder as we improve at the game), then it's a very low ROI source of points. Consequently my opponent and I have almost completely abandoned military as an option. There's still some room to be wrong, however. For example, perhaps someone who's going for a military victory should avoid completing their go-again wonders until phase III, so they can assure getting all the big military cards. That's something we don't typically do, but I've read that can be a valuable tactic.

[b]After 54 plays[/b] 

Liking the game more. We've figured out how to use Age III better so the game is more often won or lost there, and luck is less of a factor (though it's still significant). We've also learned better how to use military/science to force the opponent into uncomfortable choices. 

One thing I don't love about it still: it's very calculational at times, forcing you to do a series of nested net value calculations. That sort of intensive but basic algebra is not my favorite kind of thinking. One nice aspect of these calculations though is that they sometimes reveal counterintuitive valuations, which make for fun "aha" moments.

RATING: 8
Tiene todas las características de un euro basado en puntos de victoria con draft y set collection, estando a la altura de juegos de mayor enjundia. Esas características le dan un incentivo a la planificación a largo plazo. Además, ello se mezcla con mecánicas de tira y afloja como en el combate y en la tecnología. Sin embargo, está muy lejos del carácter solitario de muchos euros. Aquí la tensión es máxima y la interacción también porque cuando juega el adversario, te preguntas se se dará cuenta de la jugadaza que le puede dar una ventaja importante.

El draft mezclando las cartas ocultas y conocidas da mucho juego porque te permite proyectar un poco al futuro y te permite jugar con los turnos previendo que tu adversario no se lleve determinada carta.

Mi único pero es que temo que al jugar muchas veces esté un poco dirigido. Tiendes a hacer movimientos similares. Después de jugar 4 partidas, consigo hacer buenas puntuaciones proviéndome bien de recursos y luego yendo a acumular puntos y contrarrestando que el adversario no pueda hacer los suyos.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Habe das Spiel auf der Spiel 2015 getestet. Mein Fazit nach dieser einen Partie:  7 Wonder für 2 Spieler? Ja genau! Das Regelwerk und auch der gesamte Aufbau wurden genau dafür optimiert. Das ist meiner Meinung nach auch prima gelungen. Die Punkte aus dem Original die mit 2 Spielern gar nicht oder nur sehr leidlich funktionieren wurden gut gelöst. Statt Karten weiterzugeben, werden die Karten eines Zeitalters nun teilweise offen, teilweise verdeckt nach einem bestimmten Muster überlappend in der Tisch-Mitte ausgebreitet. Sobald eine Karte nicht mehr von anderen Karten überlappt wird, wird sie ggf. aufgedeckt und kann von da an von dem Spieler am Zug für eine der üblichen drei Optionen genutzt werden, als Gebäude bauen, abwerfen um Geld zu bekommen oder für den Bau an einem der Wunder. An vielen Stellen sind es nur Kleinigkeiten die angepasst wurden, aber diese Anpassungen sind gut aufeinander abgestimmt und funktionieren in Summe sehr gut. Für Leute die häufig zu zweit spielen eine klare Kaufempfehlung.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
We play 7 Wonders Duel at least once every couple of weeks. The game play is engaging and there are many paths to victory. Just when you think you have a solid strategy, you see your opponent finding another way to win the game that you didn't account for! We haven't yet played the original 7 Wonders but we've heard this is similar in part, but different enough than it's big brother. 

In all, it's a fabulous two-person game that takes about an hour or so to play. We continually want to find different ways - different paths to victory ... and that what makes this game challenging. I highly recommend it if you want to start learning about collecting wood, clay and other supplies you need to conquest your Wonders - and emerge victorious!

RATING: 8
 I picked up 7 Wonders Duel at the recommendation of my Geek Chat League (yay Beehive!). This was recommended to me by several members as being a good little two player game that offered interesting decisions but wasn't too complicated.

Well, they were bang right.

Mrs Fish and I have really enjoyed the game so far and I can see us getting a lot more play in the years to come. Really good gameplay, quite distinct from the original 7 Wonders, and great art. The only minor quibble is that the cardstock could be of better quality but that can easily be fixed with a bit of sleeving.

UPDATE:

A definite keeper. Plays well and now we both know it a good option once the Little Fish are abed.

RATING: 7
It is interesting to compare the graphic design of the cards here to that of the original 7-Wonders.  In this game, the cards are elegantly designed so that you can stack up the cards, exposing only their mechanically significant icons.  This facilitates gameplay at the expense of theme.  I don't feel like I'm building a civilization, just exposing some symbols.  The game is strategically, tactically and mechanically sound, but it  doesn't excite me or carry me away to it's game world.

RATING: 10
One of the absolute best games for two people.

It follows the theme from the original game, but works differently.

The rules are fairly easy, especially if you played the original game, the playthrough is quick (~30 min), and the decisions are interesting and meaningful.

When both players know the rules and are experienced, each turn can sometimes take less than a couple of seconds, so it's very smooth and very rare that you just sit around and wait for the other player.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
7W2 has it all. A new drafting mechanics, that makes sense in two players (unlike exchanging of hidden hands), streamlined mechanics of army and science (both allowing sudden victory DURING the game, so another thing to watch out for), perfectly short and fast-paced length, a little bit of randomness in setup to keep it replayable... and even a little bit better looks than its namesake ancestor.

Any fans of 7 Wonders who get to play in two players (like with spouses, for example), this is a must-buy. Any other people, at least think about it and go check out some videos. This may well be the best competitive game purely for two that I played.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Plays: About 5
Wonderful 2 player game. I like to play this game with my wife. There's some luck involved, but a lot of 7 Wonders Duel is about how good you can utilize the randomly drawn cards to your advantage. You have to approach a match with a flexible mindset. 

Science victory seems really hard to achieve, since your opponent can easily see it coming from a mile away. There's some possibility to rush a military win, however with science... it just takes some time.

When you start learning the game, losing can sometimes feel really frustrating. It gets better when you play with the expansion, as both players get some new options when your original plan doesn't work out. 

7 Wonders Duel is one of my favorite 2 player games. Will play anytime. The expansion is a must-have for this one.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.25
Puntos fuertes: Arte cuidado. Fácil de aprender y mecánica de juego original. Combina azar y estrategia de forma equilibrada. Las cartas caben perfectamente enfundadas en la caja, cosa que en los tiempos que corren no se porqué las editoriales no se dignan a estandarizar. El juego está diseñado para que puedas ganar de muchas formas y reconducir la estrategia compensando el azar. Las maravillas están bien implantadas en el juego.

Puntos débiles: Para la duración de las partidas, tiene bastante despliegue y preparación de los mazos y la colocación de las cartas.

Conclusión: Quizás de los mejores juegos para dos que hemos probado. Aunque por nuestra forma de jugar, se nos hace un poco engorroso sacarlo por el despliegue que tiene. El juego base, una vez jugado 3 o 4 veces se nos queda corto porque las cartas son las que son, aunque no deja de tener múltiples variantes. El juego es redondo, no cojea por ningún sitio. Con la expansión gana mucho. Es necesario probarlo para darte cuenta de las posibilidades que tiene. Es bastante entretenido.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
7 Wonders: Duel definitely feels similar to playing its older sibling. There are some important differences, of course (the threat of military/science insta-victories; progress tokens; the different way of drafting). But overall, the 2 games feel pretty similar. My wife & I both like 7 Wonders, so not surprisingly, we also both liked 7 Wonders: Duel. But while we do enjoy the 2P version of 7 Wonders, we enjoy Duel a bit more.

7 Wonders: Duel is a very good small-box game for 2 players which has some meat on it. There aren't very many of those, so for that reason alone I'd say 7 Wonders: Duel is a keeper.

面白い面白いと話題なので買ってしまった

超人気ゲーム7 wondersの二人専用版
一人で試しにやってみたけどたしかに面白そうだな

RATING: N/A
Very much enjoy this 2-player take on 7 wonders! It retains the interaction with neighboring players from the original, and intensifies it through the specific duel-setup. Further, 7 Wonders Duel translates the partially shared, partially hidden information of the original's drafting, but converts it into a visually pleasing play-space. 
It is not perfect - in my experience, much like in the original, a single-minded focus on either science or military will often win you the game, even before the final age, sometimes even if your opponent actively counteracts your strategy. However, this is not, in my experience, much different from the original, other than the fact that if you succeed in these military- or scientific strategies, the game ends straight away, in stead of dragging after the winner is all but decided.
All in all, if you want a 7 wonders experience for just two players, I highly recommend 7 wonders Duel, as it is both a tight and snappy game on its own, as well as miles better than the 2-player variant of the original 7 wonders

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
[b]Length: 4/5[/b] It may actually be a little too fast. It'd be nice if maybe there was one additional age in the game. It'd feel slightly less rushed and give your engine a bit more time to work for you, which is really important in a civ game. It really is only a small problem, since it's just close enough to be long enough.

[b]Theme: 4/5[/b] Not an original theme, but to streamline it this much and still have a good Civ theme is pretty surprising.

[b]Strategy: 3/5[/b] Although there is strategy, it's pretty obvious what it may be. If you're going for a military/science victory, you want to attempt to force your opponent to free a card that will achieve you victory. It doesn't take a rocket scientist to figure that out, but it can be interesting to try to figure out how you will force it. It's usually a pretty good idea to balance your civ, but in all honesty it's also usually obvious what to go for. Economic buildings are good if you're constantly short on money. Getting resources that your wonders don't produce is probably a good idea since your wonders will cover them, it's usually a good idea to get some science buildings to not only keep your opponent from achieving science victory, but also in an attempt to get a bonus. Basically, the game is well balanced, but it means that there's really not much wrong you can do.

[b]Fiddliness 2/5[/b] I will admit, trying to set up the cards in their formations is pretty obnoxious. It's not the end of the world, and it won't take much longer than you'd originally expect upon looking at the layout for the cards, but it's a bit of a pain to try and make sure the cards are set up in the right formation without sliding off of each other. You are also going to be going through ages quickly, and for this reason, setting up the cards is going to be about 15% of the actual game.

I also have to give a shoutout to the incredible insert. It's one of the best, and the game is actually kinda satisfying to put back up.

RATING: 6
7 Wonders Duel doesn't need to exist as a standalone game. A few tweaks and additions could have been made to the original 7 Wonders and they could have been packed together as a 2nd Edition.

7WD has improved the most confusing part of the original, the prerequisite system, has been cleaned up using better iconography. I'm not a fan of the downsizing of the cards to mini size, but its not a dealbreaker for me.

What is a dealbreaker is the scoring at the end of the game, same as regular 7 Wonders. That, and the absence of the card drafting that was so fun about the original, make this game not worth owning.

RATING: 7
I like the game overall. Fairly easy to teach and get into. I also like how quick it can be.

A few things I don't like:

1. Set up and take down are a bit tedious with shuffling decks, removing cards, setting up each age, etc.

2. Component quality is somewhat lacking. Cards all warp easily. Sleeving helped, but I expected better quality.

3. The randomness of the set up can result in occasional games where it can be pretty clear that a player will lose early on. Some games just resulted in a sequence of cards that no amount of strategy could have over come. It' rare but extremely disheartening when it happens.

RATING: 7.2
The components have been slightly downgraded from its big cousin. The design remains the same.

7 Wonders as a game includes many mechanisms that are not fit (or are far from ideal) for 2 players. But they have found imaginative abd brilliant ways to this conversion and they have managed to keep game feeling of the original game. This is no small feat at all!!

Draft, War, Science and Buying resources from your neighbours would have not worked as they were in the main game. They have been redesigned into new mechanisms that are quite brilliant.

What I dislike the most about this game is the lack of real choice. You don't have a lot of options, around half of the turns you will only have 1-2 cards to choose from. And most of the times, will be an evident better option for the strategy you are following. Available resource and money reduce the number of viable choices even more.

This lack of choice and the fact that some of the cards are face down create too much randmoness for me. Nonetheless, its short duration makes me willing to forgive this "issue".

First impression [url=https://boardgamegeek.com/geeklist/184429/item/4348482#item4348482]here.[/url]

RATING: 7.5
A pleasant conversion of the big game, although surely not worthy of all the hype surrounding it. A nice tableau (pinched from the old Tri-peaks Windows game, I'll be bound) emulates the deprivation strategies nicely, and the instant-win scenarios are potentially very tense as a result. The general gist of 7 Wonders runs clearly through the game without it ever feeling derivative.

Duel positively reeks of solid design and playtesting, and it's very playable, but I wouldn't put it over other 2P conversions such as ACBaS or Inland Port.

RATING: 6
[b]7 Wonders: Duel[/b] was traded away in a math trade 1/17/16. (Received El Gaucho)

[COLOR=#FF0000]A year later... thoughts and rating (10):[/COLOR]

[i]7 Duel This was one of my most anticipated games last year and it ended up being a flop. 7W is one of my son's favorites, but Duel felt nothing like the original. Neither of us cared for the alternate win conditions and while many like the drafting mechanism in Duel I did not. Getting screwed over because of a cad flip wasn't fun. In short I never felt like I was building towards something, there wasn't much progression round to round. I found Duel to be a watered down version of 7W and not a game I need to play again.[/i]

[COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (8):[/COLOR]

[i]First I have to admit that the hype surrounding this game has probably effected how I feel about it. I was expecting a similarly amaziing experience to the first time I played 7 Wonders and it actually kind of fell flat. I understand all the decisions that were made to add tension into the 2p game, but I'm not sure that I like them. 

Specifically I'm talking about the alternate victory conditions, winning via military or science. While this adds a lot of interaction and tension in the game, there are times when your hands are tied and you need to select a card you don't want because you've already let your opponent collect too much military or science. This isn't really fun or very strategic, in fact it's pretty straight forward. There is also a fair bit of randomness in the game for 2 reasons, first you have o idea what the face down cards are so your opponent can get a huge boon if the right card gets revealed, on top of that 3 cards are removed from each age at the beginning of the round. It is surprising how much those 3 cards have effected our games, in one game one of the science symbols was completely out of the game and in another I was strapped for resources the whole game because a lot of resource cards were removed from the game. 
There are ways to help mitigate the luck found in the game which I'll highlight below:

- The drafting pyramids you build create some tense decisions when choosing which card to take, but also introduces a lot of randomness since you have no idea which card(s) you'll be revealing for your opponent.
- The wonders do help mitigate the randomness of the game, especially the wonders that give you another turn.
- You can mitigate a lack of resources if you focus on getting cards that have the upgrade symbols.
- The guilds are quite powerful because you can choose whether to use your tableau of cards or your opponents when figuring out the VP/coins that are generated.

Where does that leave me, I'm really not sure. Considering how much my family enjoys 7 Wonders (one of my son's favorites) I'm willing to give 7W Duel more plays and my gut feeling is that the more I play the less random the game will feel. In the end I think I'll always prefer 7 Wonders, but it is nice to have a quick, 2 player version in our collection. It'll be interesting how I feel about this one a year from now.[/i]
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
規則:
*戰爭版塊放置兩位玩家中間，上面放對應版塊跟標記(中間)，隨機抽5個科技，每人7元
*12張奇蹟卡洗勻，發4張，起始選1，次家選2，起始在拿1，在重複一次但是次家先拿(第一次完有建議組合)
*每個時代拿走三張卡，然後隨意抽3張工會卡放在時代3
*當要建設時如果有缺資源每個要付2+(對手擁有的褐色灰色張數)給銀行來蓋(可能被黃建築影響)
*某些建築有對應符合可免費蓋高級建築
*遊戲總共進行三個時代，有三種勝利方式(有兩種會提前結束遊戲)，三個時代結束比分數
*先根據時代建構中央牌庫，某些蓋著某些開
*每人輪流動作直到時代結束，每次動作挑一張卡，然後三動作選1
1.建設該卡，付資源擺在面前
2.棄該卡拿2+黃色卡片金額的錢，棄牌堆是公開資訊
3.建設奇蹟，付資源卡插在你要建設的奇蹟上，場上最多七個奇蹟，第八個沒蓋的要棄掉
*如果奇蹟讓你獲得額外一回合但是時代結束(最後一張卡拿走)，則該回合失去
*拿完牌後如果有蓋著的牌可拿則打開
*所有牌拿完該時代結束，下時代牌放玩後，武力弱的決定下回合起始玩家，平手則由拿上時代最後一張牌的人起始
*每個武力可以讓玩家前進武力軌一格，到對應格對方玩家掉錢，如果走到底則武力獲勝，否則最後計分看上面最高分數
*當獲得兩個一樣科技符號時可拿一個科技圓片，收集了6個不同科技直接獲勝
*最後分數，武力軌標記在的位置對應分數加上卡片/奇蹟/科技圓片分數，每3元1分，平手藍色建築卡分高獲勝

RATING: 9
Having never enjoyed 7 Wonders any of the times I've played, I was skeptical of this two-player version. But I really liked this iteration! Maybe what's missing from the main version is that there is no clear indication to what I'm working towards or who's in the lead. Duel reveals some cards but most cards are public knowledge and your strategy comes from pre-empting your opponent. I love how the military mechanic is handled here, as a valid way to win the game. I was fully aware of my decisions during each turn and knew what I was aiming at; what cards to keep away from my opponent and what cards I forced into his hand. Instead of card drafting (which is messy and confusing in a seven player game), every card is laid out in a mahjong-style, having access to some cards and only being able to see others. In my humble opinion, this is what 7 Wonders intended to be: easy to learn, easy to strategize, and fun to play. Loved this game!

RATING: 8
Very nice implementation. The need for more money and more throwouts (vis a vis the original) surprised me a little, but is compensated by the extra cards available each age. The military and science instant-win capabilities are inspired, providing threats during the game that you must monitor, which immediately provides more interaction and opponent-deprivation plays than the original does. A good thing in a 2 player game. It reminds me of the multiple-win-races provided by one of my favourite 2-player abstract games, Aton, but this has the advantage of being laced with theme, making it more attractive to non-gamers. It's very likeable.

RATING: 7
15 plays - Preferred this over 7 wonders. Have enjoyed this with my partner on and off for a long time. There's a bit of replayability there too, with different wonder combinations and science tokens. The gameplay is quite fast and addictive.

However, I originally rated this an 8, but after a half dozen plays put it down to a 7. This was because, even though there are 3 routes to victory (a big appeal of the game), the military and science victories are not very viable after some basic familiarity with the cards. The science and military is still useful as it impoverishes your opponent (sometimes in critical moments) and gets you special abilities, but they are not victory routes. The problem is that if you actively pursue these victory types, your opponent is probably going to get all the 'point' cards while quite easily purchasing just enough of the 'other cards' to stop you from achieving your alternate goal(s). Unfortunately, by the time your science/ war plans are fully thwarted (with relative ease), there's little way for you to recover when the end game scores.

Overall, this doesn't make it a bad game at all - it is still quite good. The fact that those alternate victory conditions are there, does mean that players do need to stay aware of opponent purchases as oppossed playing their own game, making gameplay more interactive, variable and just a little more deep. If these win conditions were more viable though, this could have been one of the great 2p games.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
7 Wonders: Duel is a two-player game based on the award-winning drafting game 7 Wonders.  Instead of passing a hand of cards around to draft them, cards are arranged in a central display such that the corners of some cards are on top of other cards.  Cards cannot be taken unless they have no other cards on top of them.  Actually, the similarities to 7 Wonders are mostly thematic, as the mechanics of most of the card types has changed.  The military cards work as a tug-of-war which provides various bonuses as the marker advanced on the track.  The science cards can be turned in for a special ability if you can play two with the same symbols (and there are many more different symbols).  In any case, the thing I enjoyed most about this game was taking cards such that my opponent didn't have access to the cards I'd need later on.  I thought the game was pretty good.

(1 play)

RATING: 8
A well-designed adaptation that feels like 7 Wonders while offering original gameplay. Lots of nice elements which don't appear in the multi-player game: You can buy any resources (rather than just ones your neighbors have), there's a struggle over the tempo to make cards you need uncover when you can get them (rather than having all cards from a hand available at once), and there are some conditions to win immediately (rather than always being about points in the end).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Good game at first - many ways to win, however it fades quickly as its warts become known:

Of the dozen or so games, two of them went way out of bounds statistically where one player got lucky with early "card-reveals" and so quickly gained the resources to dominate; leading to an almost comical runaway scenario. Most games were relatively balanced, but those two games were such runaways that the winner (once me, once my wife) felt awkward, saying: "I didn't deserve to win by that margin".

Also - the game has some "dick moves" in the form of attack cards that remove critical resources from a competitor causing their engine to stall. Attack cards that cost strength or money can be interesting, but attack cards that destroy something a person has built are... not fun.

Also - there are 8 wonders in each game, only 7 of which can be built which means one player cannot build all their wonders. It's advantageous to be the one who builds all four wonders, and once both players realize this, the entire first half of the game becomes a sprint to build all four, which dominates game play and largely obsoletes any focus on science or military. After ten or so plays, the game becomes a race to build four wonders and then spend the rest of the game grabbing random blue point-cards or using attack cards to strip the opponent's engine.

I've never had a game be so fun on the first few plays and fade so fast. Once we figured the game's dominant win conditions it fell off a cliff.

RATING: 7
I like 7 Wonders Duel. It is a nice take on a 2 player drafting game which otherwise doesn't work all that well. It is fun to play and build up your civilization and try to compare it to your opponents. 

But while all of that  is true, I'm not so sure on how much strategic depth this one offers. Certainly there is some skill in the game, but I feel that the 3 cards removed from each age are a poor design decision that negates the ability to strategically plan ahead for what might be coming. I suppose the main reason for this is so a player can't deduce what a face down card actually is, but I'm not a big fan of how the removed cards can vastly change the feasibility of a military or scientific victory. 

I'll play this sometimes, but there are many other 2 player games that I like more.

Wow, didn't expect to like this one as I don't care for 7 Wonders.

RATING: 7
Pros:
1. Minimal luck - luck mostly revolves on what happens to be revealed when buying cards. This can cause some swings in the game, but not overly much.
2. Quick Play - First time playing with teaching took less than 30 minutes.
3. Simple - Rules are fairly straightforward. 

Cons:
1. One Strategy - I have a limited number of plays, but it seems you must concentrate on production and economy cards early in the game as there are a limited number of them in round 2 and none of them in round 3.
2. Science Icons - There are a very few of the science icons that can be matched up. Only 2 or 3 of each. This makes it extremely difficult to get 2 of them. Making the bonus coin mechanic not very useful. Also, requiring 6 different ones to win the game is silly. The points should escalate up with each different one that is gained, like stone age and the artifacts. Some people may like the shoot the moon aspect of it, but I thought it was a put off as it's easy to identify and easy to stop.
3. Military - Useless. Don't bother concentrating on this early, as it suffers from the family feud effect. The third round provides many military cards that are worth 2 moves making going after military in the first 2 rounds almost useless.

Overall Summary: As far as 2 player games go, this is actually a pretty good game despite my several issues with it. It's an engine building game that plays quickly. It's fast enough that I'm not overly concerned about many of my strategy complaints. Play it once very quickly and then move on to other games for the night.

RATING: 7
"Duel" is a very good reimplementation of the original "7 Wonders" - if you love the latter and want to play with your spouse or a friend, definitely pick it up. While the draft mechanic was changed to something very different, the feeling of the original is still here - plus, being able to see half of the cards (AND being unable to see the other half) introduces some new tactics and strategies. It's beautiful, it's quick, can be very tense when you're going head to head with your opponent and building you own little civilization is always fun.

Still, for me it's only a 7. A bit too light and a bit too repetitive for my personal taste, but enjoy it very much from time to time - especially when I'm not in the mood for heavier games.

RATING: N/A
2P only.

Instead of card drafting, you just pull cards off the table.  Picking up some cards, makes cards underneath them available to your opponent (some cards are face-down until they are revealed by picking up the cards that partially cover them).  You can do 3 things with cards you pick up:
  1) pay the cost to build into your city,
  2) discard for $2 (+$1 for every yellow card in your city)
  3) use to build a wonder (if you can pay the cost of the wonder)

You can win via military, or via science.  If neither of these creates a victory, then most points (based on buildings and wonders constructed) wins the game.

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
This is a solid game.  It's a go-to for 2 player games for my wife and I.  There is a bit too much luck involved in the layout of the tiles for me to rate it this high given the base rules.  With the Pantheon expansion, however...wow!

In the base game, if you are simply unable to get a decent collection of material cards in era 1, then you're going to hate eras 2 and 3.  To combat this we've implemented the following house rule, which has worked well for us.

[b]House Rule:[/b]
[i]At the end of the first era count the difference between BASIC material cards between the players.  

If player 1 had 2 basic material cards and player 2 has 5 basic material cards the difference is 3.  In this case player 1 will be able to chose 3 face-up BASIC material cards shown in era 2 and mark them with a coin from the bank.

The marked cards are only available for player 1.  However, player 1 must choose the marked cards if able as their highest priority.  This is to prevent player 1 from using those marked cards as a strategic blocker for player 2.[/i]

Using this house rule we've been able to ensure tighter scores.  We've run into several games where there is a certain winner by the end of era 1.  It's not poor planning or judgment by the losing player - sometimes the cards just come out so that one player gets destroyed!

The Pantheon expansion completely solves this problem, for us, because you have more opportunity to move in and out of the "pyramid" and prevent getting cornered into poor choices.

The ratings below are [i]with[/i] the Pantheon expansion.
:star::star::star::star::halfstar: [b]Components[/b]
:star::star::star::star::halfstar: [b]Artwork[/b]
:star::star::star::star::star: [b]Mechanics (general gameplay "fun")[/b]
:star::star::star::star::nostar: [b]Rules (easy to learn/teach)[/b]
:star::star::star::star::nostar: [b]Replay Value[/b]
:star::star::star::star::nostar: [b]Player Uptime[/b]
:star::star::star::star::nostar: [b]Meaningful Decisions (luck factor)[/b]
:star::star::star::star::nostar: [b]Total[/b]

RATING: 8
On the whole, the game is fantastic. I really enjoy how the reveal mechanic requires strategy, but doesn't drag out the game via AP. I also very much appreciate the multiple ways in which one can win - it keeps things interesting throughout. My only real complaint lies with luck. It's not necessarily an inappropriately high amount - just slightly more that I ideally prefer. Still a great game though.

RATING: 8
I quite liked the original 7 Wonders. The game was marred only by the fact that it's 2 player variant sort of sucked. Imagine my excitement when I saw this game! 

The best part is: 7 Wonders: Duel delivers. This is a tight and tense card game that's easy to pick up and tough to put down. On and off the table in less than an hour, and ALWAYS some ensuing conversation thereafter. The Majhong-type mechanic that drives the game is unique and intuitive, yet not overbearing. My only problem is, the base game gets tiring after a while. I don't really feel like I NEED to play this again. Luckily the Pantheon expansion is more than enough to bump this up to a light 9.

If you play predominantly 2-player games, this game is a must-have. It's a bit cutthroat, so it's not for everyone, but it's always got a spot in my collection.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
the overlaps with hidden cards is just brilliant and brings me back to all those summers watching grandma cheat at pyramid solitaire ("oh, drat! what have i uncovered?"). you always have some slight idea what's coming next and only ever open up a little more information for your opponent, but every single reveal could be just what they needed to scrape by. you're trying to be the best at gathering gold, resources, or connections to sponge up civic buildings and wonders, all the while tantalized with the option to threaten serious war or science. but, your opponent can see you trying to end the game early! this just leads to more delicious decisions between whether to buy the thing, block the other thing, or just stall for time. my favorite, favorite mechanism in gaming is when you must leave something juicy behind for your opponent in order to fulfill your own goals, and that's exactly what Duel does with every action. such tension, such agony! this is my kind of 2-player interaction! however, this game is not without my least favorite thing in gaming: "take-that." some wonders allow you to destroy your opponent's buildings and war allows you to steal money. those things aside, even blocking in this game doesn't feel overly mean because there's always another choice. for such a limited number of things you can do each tactical turn, it's surprising how fondly i look back to this game as one with achievable strategies. plays well during a work lunch break!!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
16-10-2015. Rated after 5 plays. Very fun twist on 7 Wonders, for two. Works great.

The game works over 3 'ages'. Each Age you have a pyramid or geometrical grid of overlapping face-up and face-down cards you can draft. You can only draft cards that are face-up, and have no cards on top of them. As soon as a card is fully uncovered, they open up.

In your turn, you can build cards, by drafting and paying resources. Built buildings give you VPs, military power, technology and resources. You can build wonders, big cards that you drafted at the start of the game and give you VPs and abilities.

There are 3 ways to win, VPs, military or tech. If your opponent ever crushes your civilization by drafting WAY more war-cards, or crushes your Civilization by drafting at last 6 different technologies, they auto-win. 

I really like it and my GF is a big fan too.

RATING: 6
I'm interested in playing this more, but I was a bit disappointed after my first play due to being overhyped. I love 7 Wonders, but not because it is the best game ever but because it fills the niche of fast-playing, high-player count strategic games that is sparsely populated by any other good games.  Duel definitely improves upon the 2-player variant of 7 Wonders, and I probably will pick it up to play with my wife or son, but it doesn't do anything amazing for the densely populated fast-playing 2-player game market.

Update: Yeah, I just bleh - mediocre mass appeal.  I'm not going to buy this one.  I find the card tree a bit predictable and boring - I tend not to like that dynamic where I am forced to take a card to let you get access to the next card.  The opposite of drafting, where you pass a hand hoping your opponent doesn't take the 2nd card you really wanted, and don't know if they'll know how much you want it or not.  Fairy Tale is straight-up better.  Better drafting dynamics, plays well with 2 but also more.  At this point in my collection, it seems a 2-player-only game has to be amazeballs and unique for me to consider buying it.

RATING: 7.5
Type: Strategy

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
:thumbsup: The game has a few clever ways in its set-up to ensure that every game plays out differently (Wonder selection, placing and unlocking a 'structure' of building cards, different science chits, etc.).
:thumbsup: 7 Wonders: duel refines the graphic design of its older brother 7 Wonders.
:thumbsup: It takes up more table space than its box makes you think it does, but it's still a great game to take with you on vacation.
:thumbsup: The three paths to victory almost feel like a mini-game. In a game with two players of roughly the same skill level, victory will probably be decided on points. This entices you to push yourself a bit more to try and win a military or science victory, without lagging behind on points. All this keeps you 'on the edge'.

----

:thumbsdown: Downside of the three paths to victory is that a more experienced player will easily defeat a beginner. On the other hand, this speaks volumes about the game's luck factor too.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Visual Appeal: (4)
The box is pleasantly small, and the components all share an art style with the original 7 Wonders.

Components: (3)
Linen finish on the score tracker but not on the cards. Cardboard is of average thickness. Standard cardboard chit coins. I usually abhor tiny cards, but I can tolerate them in this game, since it allows for a convenient smaller box size. 

Theme: (3)
Being able to construct multiple wonders feels more thematic I guess. Executed more through art than through gameplay. 

Learning: (4)
About a 60/40 text-to-picture ratio in the rule book. 10 small pages cover the components, setup, and gameplay. I found it very easy to teach. Those who have played 7 Wonders before can pick it up without hesitation.

Gameplay: (3)
I feel more able (compared to 7 Wonders) to plan the expansion of my board since I can see farther ahead right from the beginning of each round. It is still essentially just a simple question, "do I get what I need or remove what I think my opponent needs." Absent is the issue with large player counts in 7 Wonders where I am unable to affect those sitting far from me.

Overall Fun: (3) 
It's good, but not great. My fiancee and I played it a lot for a few months, but now it is starting to cool off and feel samey. I look forward to trying it with other opponents to see if maybe we just know each others' strategies too well.

RATING: 8
While always respecting what a good design 7 Wonders is, I've never really warmed to it like others around me. It's always been a great lighter game to bring out with six or seven players and quietly go about doing your thing without much tension or drama. 7 Wonders: Duel adds the tension and drama to the franchise. It's a lovely little see sawing battle between two players this time, using the same general mechanics of its older sister, which makes it very easy to pick up and dive into.

I'm still a little uncertain how much control I have over proceedings, but as long as there is at least the illusion of control then it's a cracker of a 30 minute game and very enjoyable.

RATING: 9
So far I've really enjoyed 7 Wonders: Duel. There is a lot more tension than 7 Wonders. Military and science are more thematically grounded, add more strategy to the game, and are more exciting than in 7 Wonders. I like the tension between trying to complete your wonders before your opponent so that you can complete all 4, and saving your wonders for when they will have the greatest impact. The wonders that give military or an extra action in particular can put a lot of pressure on your opponent. There is more randomness in 7 Wonders: Duel than in 7 Wonders with 3 players, and I'm not sure how well I'll regard 7 Wonders: Duel as a strategy game in comparison long term, but I'm impressed with what I've seen, and I've had a lot of fun.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This sat on my shelf for months. I bought it on a whim and every time I opened the box I struggled to get enthused about playing it. Well, as luck would have it the kids were banned from electronics and it was raining. It took about an hour to trudge through the rules, answer endless unrelated questions with an easily distracted eight year old, and finally we started playing.

Wow. I loved it. We played it three times on the weekend and I am smitten. The mechanic for laying out the buildings and thus limiting what you can and cant build is the star of the show. It makes each game unique and fun to look at. The game also succeeds in getting that feel of creating an engine that allows better purchases later on more easily. When playing with a child you can subtly influence the result with your building selection so it is enjoyable for them while they develop their skill.

Not perfect. The calculation for trading resources is a bit cumbersome. Winning by science and by military feel anti climactic.

As a game/activity for two people it is fun. As a conduit for spending time with my son, it helped make a memorable weekend. Can't wait to show it to more people and then over commit to those blue buildings again.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

The more you play you realize how very little strategy there is. Very linear gameplay with no hard decisions to make on your turn

RATING: 9

RATING: 8
By far the best 2-player game I've ever come across. I usually hate luck, but the card drafting mechanism in this game make the luck seem insignificant (all though, it probably is not). I introduced my "I-hate-board-games" girlfriend to it and she actually suggested a replay after the first round; believe me, that itself should give it a gold star! 

For the game itself: It is a quick 2-player game, with very easy learning curve. after 3 turns, you will know the game mechanisms. it takes about 3.5 minutes to explain the game rules and is therefore perfect to introduce new players to. Theme is great, as the artwork is beautiful, and you will undoubtedly have some discussion over which wonder is the best (not in gamepoint value, but in sense of which actual wonder you prefer).

Have fun with this game!

RATING: 9.5
One of the best 2-player games, and better than the multiplayer version. It completely gets the feel of 7 Wonders, but with a fresh twist. The cards are somewhat visible, and uncovering cards adds to the tension. The military track is nicely done, and also can really put the pressure on an opponent if they ignore it. The game is well-balanced and rarely felt like there was tit-for-tat that plagues many two-player games.

Also, the Pantheon is a really nice addition to the game. I think it solves some of the problems of not being the one to turn over cards and reveal them to your opponent.

RATING: 7
1 play, late prototype

The game is still in development, but I like it -- and I'm not even particularly a fan of the original game! In this version it is much easier to see what the other player is doing, and the change they made to replace drafting works quite well -- especially since you no longer have to try to remember what cards you passed! Each era's cards are placed in a display of alternating face up/down cards, in a different pattern each era. Players alternate taking them, so you have some planning based on a little foreknowledge. As usual, you can use the card for money, build it, or build a wonder (of which only 7 are available), or turn it in for money. There's even military stuff going on, simplified to a little track sitting between the players.

Good version!

RATING: 6
An absolutely wonderful little game. Unfortunately, I haven't played the original 7 Wonders, so I can't compare them, but this is a great game for 2 players. It's fairly simple to get into, and plays pretty fast, but is still very strategic.

At the moment of writing, I've played this four times with my wife, and she has won three of those games. (Need I say that this game is very high on her list of favourites?)

In short, this is a small and relatively fast game, that still has the feel of a much bigger and longer playing strategy game.

Update 2017-10-06: This is still a really good design, but I'm pretty bored with it by now. Adjusted rating accordingly.

RATING: 6
It's...fine.  There are a few interesting decisions to be made, mainly in your various opportunities to deny your opponent cards they will need, or their opportunity to flip new cards.  However, I feel that the theme and grandiosity of the game is lost in every step of the process.  The game is a sea of icons, and the character of the cards and each players civ is lost to stacking and counting three wood, two glass, etc.  There's no excitement in building a grand majestic wonder, but simply in getting more icons.  When all the cards are a collection of icons, none of them are interesting, and neither is your civilization.  You don't win because you have a beautiful mecca of cultural pleasures, you win because you have more cards with a blue header.  It comes off as soulless.  Your civ will be indistinguishable from your opponents.  You do not build an engine, you do not take on a larger strategy, instead you spend each turn picking the best general option available and working your way towards an unlikely military/science victory or an entirely underwhelming and tedious points win.  This game fills a nice void of mildly complex two player games in the 30 minute playtime window, but I don't think it fills it well.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Very clever and vastly superior adaptation of 7 Wonders to a 2p format.

To duplicate the thrill of card drafting, cards in each age are laid out in a specific structure (such as a pyramid), some face down, with cards covering those higher up. Covered cards can't be taken, but visibility into what is coming (and what could be useful to the enemy) allows for long-term planning.

All of the other familiar 7 Wonders mechanisms are there, but with enough adaptation to work with two players (resources are always available to buy, for example, but if the opponent produces them then they become more expensive).

Additional thrills come from several immediate win conditions, such as scientific advancement or military power, and new abilities enabled by the new format (such as bonus turns).

There is already a vast improvement simply by virtue of the smaller number of players; it plays faster and is more strategic. All of its other little improvements are just icing on the cake.

RATING: 10
45 minutes for a very deep two player game.  My wife can't get enough of thrashing me at this.  Love it.

I've upgraded this to be my only number 10.  I had a game the other night against my wife which was probably my most fun board gaming experience ever.  We got to the third age and I had three science symbols and she was one space from a military victory.  I had zero wonders and she had 3, with a repeat turn wonder as her fourth wonder.

As I was behind in military I got to go first.  I realised that every face down card could screw me and if there was a face up military that was hidden behind one card I was screwed.  Almost every move I made was going to blow me up.  I had a stack of cash and I went first and built every single wonder I had, removing at least one military card from the possibility of play.  It also took out my wife's ability to replay a turn.  One of those wonders allowed me to get a card from the discard pile, so I grabbed the once science card I didn't have (there was no military).  A turn in I acquired my fifth science card.

The entire age, my wife and I were planning out every move, counting all the cards left.  A face down non-guild card would annihilate us.

It came down to the last three cards.  There was one card that wasn't revealed and I had to reveal it.  There was still one third-age military card that hadn't been revealed.

And it flipped up favourably.  I was safe!  It was incredible.  The tension, the excitement, the planning and it came down to a 25% chance of being defeated right there and then.  I won by a civilian victory!  What a game.  What an incredible design.

RATING: 7
The card pyramid is AWFUL; it's a horrible, contrived mechanism in my opinion without real purpose. Often it results in a player having only one or two choices on his turn.  I like more choices and depth.  We've house-ruled this game to completely ditch the pyramid and allow card drafting a la Through the Ages -- cards are arranged in a line with some able to be purchased for free, then some for one gold, then some for two gold, and this line of cards shifts after each player's turn (with the ones at front recycled to the end of the line).  This opens up the game considerably and makes it VASTLY more strategic.  We've added other House Rules to fix other slight imbalances, especially considering that we also play with the expansion, but that is the main change.  Without our House Rules, I'd give this game a 5 or a 6.  With them, it's a 8 or 9, so I'll compromise and give the game a 7 rating.

RATING: 5
Played this in a game night with Morels and Targi. This had my highest expectations but I found it rather mediocre. OK, it is a nice adaptation of the 7 Wonders game, but does away with (arguably) the best part of that game, which is the hand-drafting. Replacing it with some dull positional play which tries to imitate but simply cannot replace a good draft and has too much of an influence during play.

As I find 7 Wonders ok but am not too thrilled about it, this actually falls down from ok to mediocre and would definitely would play a lot of other 2p games over this one. I can see this game targeted to non/starting gamers with not so many games in their collection, as it is accessible, has some engine going in it, multiple scoring possibilities and big replayability. 

But I can see past the chrome and see a simple set collection game with artificial constricted choices and very few interaction (even less as in 7 Wonders). Pass.

3/4/2017: This is probably one of the most played games that I currently have in my collection, though the play stat may not reflect that since I did not actively track my plays until recently. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
7 Wonders Duel is aptly named. This is not a case of two players merrily building their own civilizations and simply comparing scores at the end. Rather, its a game where every action can have great consequences for both players, and you definitely need to keep an eye on what your rival is doing. While the opportunities for directly affecting your opponent are fairly limited (a couple of wonders allow you to destroy one of your rival's resources and military attacks and some wonders can let you ding them for coins), the main card drafting mechanic is as much about what defense as it is building your own empire. It can, in fact, be a bit mean. Providing you have an opponent who knows it's all in fun, 7 Wonders Duel is about as deep, challenging and satisfying as a 30-minute game gets.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This game does everything that I want a 2-player game to do. Fast play time, plenty of tough decisions, multiple end-game conditions, some slight asymmetry (after you build some wonders and science tokens).

To me it feels like the 7 Wonders equivalent of Agricola: All Creatures Big and Small. That game perfectly distilled Agricola down to a 20 minute, strategic 2-player experience. 7 Wonders: Duel does the exact same thing. It feels like 7 Wonders because of the way resources work and the card types are familiar (blue card give points, brown card resources, etc) but there are several enhancements that make it a much tighter, better 2-player experience.

I just don't rate this game higher for a couple of reasons. One if the start player advantage. To me the game just feels like there is a start player advantage because you can control the initial momentum of the game, and data posted on the forums shows a definite higher start player win rate. It seems like player two should start with one or two extra coins.

I also don't like that a player can control the game to get what they want. Since you and your opponent each have to remove a card on each turn, you can play in a way that you are guaranteed to get the science/military card that you need just based on how the cards are randomly laid out. That can be anticlimactic.

Update: The expansion seems to improve the game greatly and it addresses my second issue, where players are able to get the card they need most with no way to stop it. Now there is a new action that doesn't require you to remove a card, so you can stall and change up the order by taking that action.

The expansion also gives you incentive to reveal face-down cards. In the base game you would try to never do this because it might give your opponent the perfect card. But now some of the face-down cards have nice bonuses on them, and if you reveal the cards you get the bonuses. This is a great addition to the game.

After more plays with the expansion I feel like my rating could go up, making this the game I wanted it to be.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
