RATING: 6
Japanese/English version
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I've been sort of played out on regular 7 Wonders for a while now... especially as the one guy who likes to play it most wants to include a bunch of expansions every time.  And I don't often play 2 player board games (outside of Go which is it's own thing).  So I had a little trepidation about this game, but it was a convenient time for a two player game and it looked like it could be interesting.

There are a number of changes from the original, but the biggest is that it's not really a drafting game.  (There is a draft for wonders at the beginning of the game, but that's not a large part of the game).  Instead, cards are played in a special pattern to the table.  In the first era, the cards form a pyramid.  (See picture)

[ImageID=2558121]
Only cards that aren't covered by other cards can be taken.  You can either take a card, by paying for it, discard it for money (which works a little differently than the original game) or use it to build one of your wonder cards.  Some of the cards will be face down and will only be revealed when they become uncovered.

What this means is that there's a real dance sometimes, trying to avoid uncovering a card that your opponent wants.  Or trying to avoid uncovering the face down cards, as that might give your opponent first crack at some awesome cards.  In particular, there are a couple of ways that a player can just "win instantly" which relate to getting military or science cards... so in the late game if your opponent has pursued those paths, revealing an unknown card could prove to be deadly.  Since many of the cards will be face up, you can often plan ahead if there is a card you really want, or really want to deny your opponent.  I found this aspect of the game to be more compelling than the drafting mechanism in the original game.  It really worked quite well and made for an interesting game.

There were a couple of other important changes to mention.  When discarding a card for money, you get a base amount of 2 coins plus an extra coin for every yellow card in your tableau.  When buying a resource it costs a base 2 coins plus an extra coin for every good of that type that your opponent has that you don't.  So if you need a clay and your opponent produces 3 and you produce 1, then it'll cost you a base of 2 plus 2 more for the difference between your production and your opponent's production.  That money gets paid to the bank though, not to your opponent.  And you can always buy goods, even if neither you or your opponent make them.

Military works differently. There's a military track that works kind of like a reverse tug of war.  Whenever you play a military shield, you push the military marker closer to your opponent.  The further you push it towards him, the worse it is.  If you push far enough he loses first 2 coins, then 5 coins.  And if you push all the way to the end, you simply win the game immediately.  Failing that, you'll get points at the end of the game depending on how far you pushed the marker.  There are three levels you can achieve 2 pts, 5 pts and 10 pts.

Science works completely differently as well.  There's not set collection scoring wonkiness.  Some of the cards give you points, but mostly just piddly amounts.  There's still a set collection aspect though.  There's 6 different types of symbols.  If you ever collect 2 of the same type, you get to take one of the science bonus tokens that are in play in that game.  And if you ever collect all 6 symbols, then you just win immediately.  (There's a seventh symbol on one of the science bonus tokens that makes this a bit easier too).  Each game will have 5 of 10 of those tokens in play, so there's some randomness on what will be in any particular game.

The different eras get laid out in different card configurations.  A pyramid in era 1, a reverse pyramid in era 2 and a weird egg shaped thing in era 3.  In each era, three cards are randomly removed from the game, so you can't always count on every card being available.

The game was a lot of fun.  It felt very similar to 7 Wonders but also very fresh and different.  I'm not sure that it would be that easy for a new player, who hadn't played 7 Wonders, to pick up though.  But for those who know the original game, this won't be too difficult to learn.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
About [thing=173346][/thing]:

[b]1) What is it?[/b]
[thing=173346][/thing] is a reimplementation of [thing=68448][/thing] for 2 players only. Sure, [thing=68448][/thing] do have 2 players rules, using the Free City, and I played with them and they work OK. Still, if you truly want a very good 2p game, [thing=173346][/thing] is your thing.

[thing=173346][/thing] has the same feel of [thing=68448][/thing]: 3 Eras, several types of cards (scientific, civic, military, production, etc) and cards have requirements you need to pay and "chains" of buildings, so if you have and earlier version, you get for free the upgrade. Is a well known and proven mechanic, and is what you will get here. But [thing=173346][/thing] come with several changes: there isn't a draft phase anymore, as all the cards for the Era are in the table, some hidden, some open, and players can't take the hidden ones before they are open. Also, every player has 4 Wonders to build. Finally, the game can end in three ways: by finishing the cards of the 3rd Era, by Scientific victory and by Military victory.

[thing=173346][/thing] can go in a briskly pace and end in less than 20 minutes, and even longer games won't break too much the 30 minutes.

[b]2) How do you play?[/b]
After picking the Wonders, in the turn the player can:
- Build a card (paying its costs, if any);
- Build a Wonder (paying its costs);
- Discard a card for money.

That is it. The cards for the Eras are set in a way that the cards in the base row are open, then there is a row of hidden cards, blocked by the open ones bellow, and the cards are revealed only when there isn't a card blocking it.

Players can buy all kinds of goods, but they pay 2 plus the level of production of the opponent - so if I want glass, and my opponent produces 2 glass, I need to pay 4 to the bank, for each glass I need. To balance this, when you sell a card for money, you get 2 money plus one money for each yellow (merchant) card you have in your tableau.

Scientific cards don't earn points as they used to, now they can earn you science tokens, that have some strong powers, and can be a way to win the game. Military can earn points, even if you don't conquer the enemy city (miltary victory), and also causes your opponent to lose money, which can be really bothersome.

[b]3) Which are the decisions made during play?[/b]
What to build. Not only what you need, but what the other player wants. Military and Scientific victories won't just happen, so you need to start going for them right from the start, and even if you aren't going for them, leave the cards for the other side might lead you to defeat.

So there is a good balance of strategic and tatical decisions, for both sides - more than that of [thing=68448][/thing], as the 2 players limit makes the luck go down, as either you or the opponent will take certain card.

[b]4) What are the good things in the game?[/b]
- Smart, smooth and engaging mechanics;
- Quick playing time;
- Several paths to victory;
- Superb presentation, artwork and bits, all in a very reasonable sized box;
- Easy to learn, teach and play;
- Icons and symbols are mostly easy to understand;
- Mostly indirect interaction (like raising the costs of a resource);
- Language independent;
- Seems to have a good replay value, with not all Wonders being used, nor all the cards, and the order (and if in the open or hidden rows) in which the cards come also affect the game.

[b]5) Which are the bad news?[/b]
- 2 players only;
- Still, like in basic [thing=68448][/thing], not all that thematic;
- Mostly indirect interaction (raising the costs of a resource; bloking);
- There are Wonders that aren't Wonders but are, at least, nice.

[b]6) How do you feel while playing?[/b]
Sort like playing an abstract, but not so thinky, as there is luck and hidden information, so while there is room for some AP, it won't be as bad as someone looking for the "perfect move".

Since there is more blocking and indirect conflict than anything else (there are some Wonders that, when built, destroy production cards of the opponent, but that is it), it won't hurt feelings of those playing, which is an important feature considering couples playing often.

In the end, even I, a person that doesn't like competitive 2 players game, not only don't mind having played it, I actively enjoyed! [thing=173346][/thing] proved itself to me as a very good option in the 2 players field, and if happens with it what did with [thing=68448][/thing], which got better with expansions (Leaders and Cities), I can see this even improving in the future.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
7 Wonders: Duel is an engine-building game based on card drafting. Because there are only two players, often the best move is taking away a card that would help your opponent more than you, rather than simply building up your own engine. This is central in the two push-pull objectives (military and science) to keep your opponent in check.

RATING: 8
I am definitely not a huge fan of the regular version of 7 Wonders, and because of that I came very close to missing this release. Which would have been a tragedy. Duel is fantastic, and easily one of the premier 2-player games out there.

Although not as quick to pick up as something like Jaipur or Lost Cities, Duel rewards the longer learning curve with greater variance. The solitaire-y set-up for each age is fantastic and can lead to some really sweaty decisions. 

So all in all, a longer learning and playtime than you'd expect from a 2-player card game, but well worth it. More fun than 7 Wonders in my opinion.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Superb, Excellent strategic depth.  Chase a military victory, civilian, or scientific win while opposing the other player.  Fairly easy game to teach.  30 to 45 minute play time.  Requires a fairly large play area to lay out all the cards -- I don't recommend playing on a picnic table at a windy beach.

There is a solo variant available for this game under the BGG files section.  It works, but the game loses a lot in comparison to a human opponent.

I have never played the original multi-player edition.

RATING: 6
There is a familiar feel to playing Duel and regular 7 Wonders. The designers have done a good job keeping the original theme and feel of the game play. Players select instead of draft cards, but the goal remains more or less the same: to accrue points in multiple categories to win the game. The introduction of sudden death victories in science and military conquest helps shake things up, but you can see these scenarios a mile away and can easily prevent your opponent from scoring these victories.

My rating remains low as the game is mediocre at best. Player interaction is limited to denial of choice and in our plays, building is too easy and cheap. Almost every single building we wanted to build could be purchased or built without too much difficulty. So the game really boils down to card selection which is heavy on luck. It doesn't feel quite right when you can pick up Civilian buildings that score lots of VP at a lucky flip of the card and without any "costs". Wonders are also a piece of cake to build for the most part. We often finish building all the Wonders by the end of Age II. 

One positive note: the game is really low key and spouse-friendly. Though many claim it is too "take-that". I really do not think so. The game is relatively light with very little decision-making as most of the time, the choices are quite obvious. Sure, you may take a card just to deny your opponent points, but I hardly think it is vicious. 

The game is fine, just that my expectations were high due to the hype and positive response from BGG. In the end, I was fairly disappointed.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9.2
MARAVILHOSA adaptação de 7Wonders para 2 jogadores. Nunca cheguei a jogar o "pai maior" com apenas 2, mas pelo q li da regras e oq me disseram, sempre me pareceu "gambiarrado" demais. Aqui as regras foram adaptadas perfeitamente. E mesmo as regras mudadas sem razão aparente, a princípio, acabaram melhorando o jogo. A maior importância, por exemplo, para o dinheiro e as cartas amarelas foram ótimas. De cara "torci o nariz" para o fato de cada jogador não ter "a maravilha",  parecia descaracterizar o "7 wonders", mas depois, pensando bem, de fato 7 Wonders só tem 7 maravilhas com 7 jogadores, enquanto Duel.... Sempre tem 7 maravilhas passíveis de construção, então ele é o verdadeiro 7W rsrs
E oq já era ótimo como gateway, ficou ainda melhor, pq agora o "novato" não precisa mostrar suas cartas para tirar alguma dúvida, pois as cartas estão abertas para os dois. A única coisa realmente "perdida" foi o inovador fator "escravos de jó" de 7 Wonders, mas q foi substituído por algo igualmente diferente, a distribuição única das cartas a cada era.
Enfim, um jogo fantástico, "redondinho", pronto pra entrar no top 10 de qualquer um.

RATING: 5
Oct 2017: Rating -2 (to 5)
There are so many better games out there.  I bought this originally because it was a game designed for 2P and I mostly play 2P.  Made sense, right?  However, I've come to realize in the past year that there are tons of games that play up to 4-5P that play just as well, if not better, with 2.  Most games have special rules for playing with 2P and in many cases those rules make the game work fine.  There's no need to limit yourself to 2P games and, once you consider all the other games out there, this one really starts to fall short.

My cards also started showing bad wear after just 3 plays of this (without anyone being rough with the cards), so if you're buying this make sure you immediately buy sleeves.  Otherwise you'll have earmarked cards that are easy to spot even when face down.

Original, Dec 2016:
I don't think this is my favorite 2 player game, but it's not a bad game.  It has a little more complexity to it than Jaipur, with multiple paths to victory.  It plays out very fast despite having a lot to think about, which makes it a nice filler around heavier games.

While the art is nice, I don't feel like the theme pulls off as well as I'd like.  The rule book is also very confusing in some areas and it took us several tries (and some videos) to figure out how to play.  It doesn't feel as challenging as it could.  One play through I was able to construct all 4 wonders (in the first age!) before my boyfriend had any.  I then just focused on military and ended up winning by a large margin.

RATING: 4
Talk about the penultimate spouse game- thus its popularity. The learning curve is smoothed considerably by having previously played 7 Wonders, but without that prior knowledge the extent of rules overhead for such a shallow game would be astronomical. Which is yet another reason why this the opposite of my type of game- I like games where complexity emerges from simple rules, not the other way around. The best aspect of the design to me were the sudden death victory conditions, which add a bit of tension to the affair, but by and large this is just mundane drafting and more drafting with a side order of engine building. Lacking an auction or spatial element (along with shared incentives my favorite mechanics in games), Duel was never going to be up my alley.

RATING: 8
[i]Rating with the Pantheon expansion added to the base game (7/10 without the expansion).[/i]

The base game on its own is just a solid design (I was never THAT enamored with it to be honest). But once you add the Pantheon expansion Duel becomes a whole different beast, the decision tree becomes much wider and the gameplay becomes less predictable and linear.

Duel has some great new features (as compared to its older brother) - I love the special abilities granted by science tokens, they are a really nice touch and I would love to see them retroactively implemented into the original 7 Wonders.

One word of warning though - keep in mind that Duel has a lot of "take that" moments, some folks may not enoy that. If you want a peaceful game definitely stick to the classic 7 Wonders. 

[b]/update, January 2018[/b]: I've traded my copy away, my significant other didn't enjoy this game all that much so there was no point in keeping it in my collection (Duel is too casual for my gaming group).

RATING: 3
Unsatisfying follow-up to the sensational card drafting experience. There was so much potential here with cleverly expanded trade rules, card placement for draft picks and solidly maintained iconography. Sadly this game has fallen flat with almost everyone I've played it with based on the idiotic "skip turn" symptom attached to building half of the wonder cards. Taking bonus turns from fulfilling the resource order requirements on wonder cards is so slanted in favour of the player who gets to these first, that in unsettles almost all games of Duel. Whoever thought this was a good idea needs to be taken out to the barn and shot! One of few games I've researched, bought, played, taught and had to flip. Stick to the original game, it's still  one of the best.

RATING: 9
I had watched lots of reviews and play throughs of 7 Wonders: Duel before playing it, but I had a very strong feeling that I'd like it, and I can report back that I was right. Go me. 

Take all of the tension of playing 7 Wonders and ratchet it up a bit as it's a lot clearer how the other player is doing. Then add in a few additional ways to win (or lose) and now you're balancing a few things in an attempt to stay afloat. Do you go for the military win and crush your opponent? Or do you go enlightened and go for the science victory? Then again, maybe you stick it out and go for points. All of those are viable, you just have to decide what you're going to do.

RATING: 8.34
Two-player variant of 7 Wonders.  The card drafting works differently; instead of the ‘take one card, pass the rest on’ card drafting of the main game, you will have a solitaire-style layout of cards in front of both players and you can only pick up cards that are not currently covered by any other card, which feels more interesting.   There is one less standard resource (ore) and one less luxury resource (textiles) than in the base game.

RATING: 8
My first play of 7 Wonders Duel with the expansion. 

This one is tough to rate. I really enjoy 7 Wonders Duel. It's a great light-to-middleweight filler game to close out a night or fill time while waiting for others to arrive. While it isn't much of an engine builder (in fairness, what could be at the 30 playtime), the "sudden death" victory conditions add some really nice tension/decisions to card drafting. 

While the expansion adds some additional depth to the game (you can take an action that doesn't burn a card to try playing with the order you open up new cards for your opponent), it also felt like the game became more fiddly. Sure it gives you a bonus for uncovering cards (which was previously generally negative as it gives your opponent a choice of a previously-unknown card). But I'm not sure the expansion adds any can't-miss mechanics and certainly costs time in rules explanation, setup, and looking up rules.

I probably wouldn't play without it as I put everything in the base game box. Still, I wouldn't rate this as a slam dunk, can't miss expansion.

Base Game:
:star::star::star::star::nostar:

Expansion:
:star::star::star::nostar::nostar:

RATING: 8.5
(+) für Alleinstellungsmerkmal: Card-Drafting aus einer Auslage aus offenen und verdeckten Karten, die erst durch das wegnehmen verfügbar werden.
(+) für Kennervorteil / andauernde Lernkurve: Je öfter man Spielt und die Karten der Zeitalter kennt, je besser wird man. 
(+) für mehrere Siegpfade: Punktesieg, wenn kein Wissenschafts- oder Militärsieg. Außerdem können Punkte durch die Guilden verstärkt werden.
(+) für Abwechslung ohne Erweiterung: In jeder Runde liegen nur ein Teil der Bonusplätchen, Weltwunder oder Stadtkarten aus.
(+) für Strategieentwicklung: Man kann Strategien entwickeln und timen, ohne das der Glücksfaktor die Planung zunichte macht.
(+) für flexible Entscheidungen: Man kann/muss seine Strategie ändern und auf den Gegner reagieren.
(-) für beschränkte Spieleranzahl
(+) für die Kompensation des Startspielervorteils
(+) für Siegmöglichkeit per Zielbedingungen, nicht nur profanes Siegpunkte sammeln
(+) für den Aufbaumechanismus: Verstärkung der Aktionen ist durch verschiedene Elemente/Gebäude/Regeln möglich
(+) für Spannung und Konterstrategien: Es wird erst kurz vor Ende klar wer gewinnt.
(+-) für Glücksausgleich, Man hat durch Ausschluss und genügend Kenntnis einen Vorteil, kann aber letzlich nicht 100%ig sagen wo welche Karte verdeckt liegt, da immer 3 Karten aus dem Set genommen werden.
(+) für Spannung bis zum Ende
(+) für crossing paths: Nahezu alle Entscheidungen sind vom Gegenspieler abhängig.

RATING: 9
Review Date: May 2017

Positives: Excellent production, lots of depth for such a little game, strategic without being too much for its size, various winning strategies, lots of fun, plays in just the right amount of time, great value for money.

Negatives:  Perhaps the science route to victory is too difficult and only the points/military is viable, which is a shame 

Conclusion: Excellent 2 player game with a solid theme, good structure, approachable at all levels and plays in just the right amount of time for its weight

RATING: N/A
5 after 1 play

The original 7 Wonders 2-player variant is significantly better than this game. Player interaction is substantially lower and gameplay feels much less strategic. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.9
Great game with some real tension in the balance of win conditions. Solves the problem of wanting a solid two player version of 7 Wonders, happy with this one.

We've played 9 games so far and quite a few games have been decided by the luck of card flips. Starving opponents of resources seems to be an oft visited strategy in our games as we draft brown and grey cards really highly in the opening 2 ages. Sometimes one person gets well ahead on resources which punishes the other player considerably. It is a short game however so this eases the frustration. The past three games seem to have been more balanced and I think we're getting better at drafting Wonders controlling the board and making better choices - the games are really close when they go to points.

Advantages
+ Fast turns make it a very back and forth 2 player game
+ Multiple win conditions
+ Good expansion

Disadvantages
- Resource screw
- Iconography
- Card quality

RATING: 2
After 6 plays. We don't understand why this game seems to work for most people but not for us. We feel that this game is essentially a fight for resources, both for yourself to be able to pay the cards, but perhaps even more importantly to deprive the other player. In about 2/3 of the time, this ended in an imbalance, where one player had (sometimes a lot) more resources than the other,  leaving the other person with a frustrating experience, because (s)he could hardly do anything. Without enough (or the right) resources you can't build cards, you are forced to often dump cards for money, you can't build your wonders, etc. Perhaps if we played 10 more times we could learn how to play so that you always get your fair share of the resources, but it's simply not worth it for us. There are too many games out there that do work for us right out of the box.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A truly excellent small box 2 player game. Plays quickly, and is always enjoyable. There are 2-3 decisions you need to make each turn and there is always a nice level of uncertainty. What cards am i revealing for my opponent? Will he take this card or that card? 

Each game can feel different. For example last night my opponent had 3 wonders with the "take another action" icon. That was a fantastic play but i still managed to win. Quite memorable sessions for such a short game. I usually ignore Scientific and Military victories and it has cost me quite a few times. 

The more i play games the less i want to do math problems. However, i still want to feel that i am making meaningful decisions. 7 Wonders Duel is one such game.
